                    }
                }
            }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2014 47:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2014-11-24"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2014 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Zhong-Jian Liu, Lai-Qiang Huang, Yi-Bo Luo, Hong-Hwa Chen and Yves Van de Peer report the first genome sequence of a crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) plant, the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. They identify genes encoding CAM pathway enzymes and find that gene duplication was likely a key process in the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Orchidaceae, renowned for its spectacular flowers and other reproductive and ecological adaptations, is one of the most diverse plant families. Here we present the genome sequence of the tropical epiphytic orchid Phalaenopsis equestris, a frequently used parent species for orchid breeding. P. equestris is the first plant with crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) for which the genome has been sequenced. Our assembled genome contains 29,431 predicted protein-coding genes. We find that contigs likely to be underassembled, owing to heterozygosity, are enriched for genes that might be involved in self-incompatibility pathways. We find evidence for an orchid-specific paleopolyploidy event that preceded the radiation of most orchid clades, and our results suggest that gene duplication might have contributed to the evolution of CAM photosynthesis in P. equestris. Finally, we find expanded and diversified families of MADS-box C/D-class, B-class AP3 and AGL6-class genes, which might contribute to the highly specialized morphology of orchid flowers."/>

        <meta property="og:title" content="The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Zhong-Jian Liu, Lai-Qiang Huang, Yi-Bo Luo, Hong-Hwa Chen and Yves Van de Peer report the first genome sequence of a crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) plant, the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. They identify genes encoding CAM pathway enzymes and find that gene duplication was likely a key process in the evolution of CAM photosynthesis."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Orchidaceae, renowned for its spectacular flowers and other reproductive and ecological adaptations, is one of the most diverse plant families. Here we present the genome sequence of the tropical epiphytic orchid <i>Phalaenopsis equestris</i>, a frequently used parent species for orchid breeding. <i>P. equestris</i> is the first plant with crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) for which the genome has been sequenced. Our assembled genome contains 29,431 predicted protein-coding genes. We find that contigs likely to be underassembled, owing to heterozygosity, are enriched for genes that might be involved in self-incompatibility pathways. We find evidence for an orchid-specific paleopolyploidy event that preceded the radiation of most orchid clades, and our results suggest that gene duplication might have contributed to the evolution of CAM photosynthesis in <i>P. equestris</i>. Finally, we find expanded and diversified families of MADS-box C/D-class, B-class <i>AP3</i> and <i>AGL6</i>-class genes, which might contribute to the highly specialized morphology of orchid flowers.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Ever since Darwin published <i>Fertilization of Orchids</i> in 1862 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Darwin, C. On the Various Contrivances by Which British and Foreign Orchids are Fertilised by Insects (Cambridge University Press, 2011)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1135">1</a>), orchids have attracted great interest from evolutionary biologists and botanists. Orchidaceae is one of the largest plant families, with between 22,075 and 26,567 species in 880 genera (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#Sec23">URLs</a>), and is known for its diversity in specialized reproductive and ecological strategies. The specific development of the labellum (the 'lip') and gynostemium (a fused structure of the stamens and pistils) to trick pollinators and facilitate pollination is well documented, and the coevolution of orchid flowers and pollinators is well known<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Schiestl, F.P. et al. The chemistry of sexual deception in an orchid-wasp pollination system. Science 302, 437â438 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1142">2</a></sup>. In addition to the highly sophisticated floral structure contributing to the diversification of orchids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Cozzolino, S. &amp; Widmer, A. Orchid diversity: an evolutionary consequence of deception? Trends Ecol. Evol. 20, 487â494 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1146">3</a></sup>, CAM and epiphytism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Silvera, K., Santiago, L.S., Cushman, J.C. &amp; Winter, K. Crassulacean acid metabolism and epiphytism linked to adaptive radiations in the Orchidaceae. Plant Physiol. 149, 1838â1847 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1151">4</a></sup> might also be linked to the adaptive radiation of orchids.</p><p><i>Phalaenopsis</i> species are popular ornamental plants worldwide because of their elegant appearance and extended longevity, and they are of great economic importance for the floral industry. <i>P. equestris</i> is an important breeding parent because of its many colorful flowers in a single inflorescence. It has a karyotype of 2<i>N</i> = 2X = 38 with uniform small-size chromosomes of 1â2.5 Î¼m in length<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Lin, S. et al. Nuclear DNA contents of Phalaenopsis sp. and Doritis pulcherrima. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 126, 195â199 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1166">5</a></sup>. Its genome size is estimated to be 1.6 Ã 10<sup>9</sup> bp per haploid genome, which is relatively small in comparison to the genomes of other species in the same genus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Lin, S. et al. Nuclear DNA contents of Phalaenopsis sp. and Doritis pulcherrima. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 126, 195â199 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1172">5</a></sup> or even other genera<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Leitch, I.J. et al. Genome size diversity in orchids: consequences and evolution. Ann. Bot. 104, 469â481 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1177">6</a></sup>. Some transcriptome sequence data have previously been generated for this species, and a transcriptome database, OrchidBase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Tsai, W.C. et al. OrchidBase 2.0: comprehensive collection of Orchidaceae floral transcriptomes. Plant Cell Physiol. 54, e7 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1181">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Fu, C.H. et al. OrchidBase: a collection of sequences of the transcriptome derived from orchids. Plant Cell Physiol. 52, 238â243 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1184">8</a></sup>, has been established. Here we present the first whole-genome sequence and analysis of <i>P. equestris</i>, providing fundamental knowledge for further research in orchid biology.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>We adopted a whole-genome shotgun strategy to sequence and assemble the genome of <i>P. equestris</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 1</a>) and estimated the genome size to be 1.16 Gb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>), which is smaller than previous estimates by flow cytometry (<span class="stix">â¼</span>1.6 Gb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Lin, S. et al. Nuclear DNA contents of Phalaenopsis sp. and Doritis pulcherrima. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 126, 195â199 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1215">5</a></sup>. We assembled 236,185 scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 2</a>), with about 90% of the total assembled genome (<span class="stix">â¼</span>980 Mb) contained in the 6,359 longest scaffolds.</p><p>The total genome assembly amounted to 1.086 Gb (1.0 Gb without unknown (N) bases), which is <span class="stix">â¼</span>93% (<span class="stix">â¼</span>86% without N bases) of the estimated total genome size. We assessed the coverage of the genome assembly using BACs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Tables 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">4</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>). For contigs longer than 1 kb in length (979 in total) generated by sequencing and assembling BAC pools, 97% could be mapped to the assembled genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). Comparison of ten randomly sequenced BAC scaffolds indicated a low error rate (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 4</a>). Of the 248 conserved core eukaryotic genes that were used to assess genome completeness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1242">9</a></sup>, 234 (94%) were uncovered in our genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>), and a majority of transcription fragments could be mapped to the genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Tables 6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">8</a>). Further detailed information is provided in the Online <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#Sec11">Methods</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome annotation and gene expression analysis</h3><p>About 62% of the genome assembly was found to be composed of repetitive DNA, a higher proportion than the 29% in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Maere, S. et al. Modeling gene and genome duplications in eukaryotes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 102, 5454â5459 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1273">10</a></sup> and 41% in grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1277">11</a></sup> but similar to the proportion in sorghum (61%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551â556 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1281">12</a></sup>. Interspersed repeats and transposable elements (TEs) occupied about 59% of the genome, and tandem repeats accounted for 3% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 9</a>). Among the TEs, LTRs (long terminal repeats) were the most abundant and occupied <span class="stix">â¼</span>46% of the genome, followed in frequency by LINEs (long interspersed nuclear elements), which accounted for <span class="stix">â¼</span>8% of the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Tables 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">11</a>).</p><p>We analyzed the distribution of the divergence times for the complete LTRs in the genome assembly and estimated that most LTRs (71%) in the orchid genome arose during a relatively recent insertion between 11.7 and 43 million years ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>), long after the origin of the last common ancestor of orchids (74â86 million years ago)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="RamÃ­rez, S.R., Gravendeel, B., Singer, R.B., Marshall, C.R. &amp; Pierce, N.E. Dating the origin of the Orchidaceae from a fossil orchid with its pollinator. Nature 448, 1042â1045 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1301">13</a></sup>. Separate divergence analysis of the <i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i> TEs suggested that these two types of LTRs experienced a recent burst (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>).</p><p>Next, protein-coding gene models were constructed using a pipeline combining <i>de novo</i> prediction, homology-based prediction and RNA sequenceâaided prediction. In total, we predicted 29,431 protein-coding gene models, excluding genes with similarity to known TEs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 12</a>). We also predicted alternatively spliced forms of these protein-coding genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 13</a>) and found 9,021 splice variants for 6,389 genes (21.7% of the total protein-coding genes). Of the 29,431 predicted genes, 20,398 (69.3%) were supported by transcriptome data from at least 1 of 4 tissues examined (leaf, flower, stem and root), and 15,530 gene models (52.8%) were supported by all 3 prediction methods, namely, homology-based, <i>de novo</i> and transcriptome-based prediction, and are therefore considered to represent the high-confidence gene set (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). For gene model validation, we manually examined 500 randomly selected genes from 2,038 of the gene families shared among monocots that only had 1 copy in both <i>P. equestris</i> and rice (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 14</a>). Most of these 500 genes were correctly predicted (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>), although 20 contained potential annotation errors, overall reflecting an annotation accuracy of 96%.</p><p>Gene similarity clustering for the set of 29,431 predicted genes yielded 3,694 gene families containing at least 2 orchid genes. Furthermore, 4,171 orchid genes could not be grouped with any of the genes from the following speciesâ<i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>, <i>Zea mays</i>, <i>Physcomitrella patens</i>, <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i> and <i>Ostreococcus lucimarinus</i>âand are referred to as orphan genes. A 4-way comparison of orchid, rice, grape and <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>) showed that there were 5,696 gene families shared by all 4 species, and 4,775 gene families were unique to <i>P. equestris</i>, more than in <i>A. thaliana</i> (2,647) and grape (3,634) but fewer than in rice (10,905). For all species, expanded and contracted gene families (in comparison to ancestors) were compared with those of <i>P. equestris</i> to identify gene families that were only expanded or contracted in <i>P. equestris</i>. In total, 2,497 gene families were expanded in <i>P. equestris</i>, whereas only 3 gene families were contracted (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM16">Supplementary Tables 15</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM17">16</a>). For the gene families specifically expanded in <i>P. equestris</i>, we conducted Gene Ontology (GO) enrichment analysis and found enrichment for 'transition metal ion binding' and 'zinc ion binding', probably reflecting the importance of genes for the binding of metal ions (including iron and zinc) in orchid.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Evolution of P. equestris."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Evolution of <i>P. equestris</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3149/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3149/MediaObjects/41588_2015_Article_BFng3149_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3149/MediaObjects/41588_2015_Article_BFng3149_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="881"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Comparison of the number of gene families in orchid (<i>P. equestris</i>), rice (<i>O. sativa</i>), grapevine (<i>V. vinifera</i>) and <i>A. thaliana</i>. (<b>b</b>) Phylogenetic tree and gene family expansion and contraction. The phylogenetic tree was constructed from a concatenated alignment of 72 single-copy gene families from 11 green plant species. Gene family expansions are indicated in orange, and gene family contractions are indicated in gray; the corresponding propotions among total changes are shown using the same colors in the pie charts. Inferred divergence dates (in millions of years) are denoted at each node in blue. MRCA, most recent common ancestor. Blue portions of the pie charts represent the conserved gene families.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3149/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To obtain functional annotations for the coding genes, we first used InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Hunter, S. et al. InterPro: the integrative protein signature database. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D211âD215 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1455">14</a></sup> to identify known protein domains. This approach uncovered 94,693 domains encoded by 17,931 genes (60.93% of all genes). On the basis of their encoding specific protein domains, 12,739 genes were linked to 129,064 GO annotations. Second, we used high-quality functional annotations from rice, excluding GO labels inferred through electronic annotation alone, in combination with a tree-based approach to transfer these labels to orchid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Proost, S. et al. PLAZA: a comparative genomics resource to study gene and genome evolution in plants. Plant Cell 21, 3718â3731 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1459">15</a></sup>. In this way, 8,518 new or more specific GO labels were assigned to 3,090 genes. The combination of both approaches resulted in a total of 13,954 genes (47.41%) with GO terms assigned to them (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 17</a>).</p><p>To quantify general gene expression levels, we mapped all RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) data to the annotated genes and calculated RPKM values (reads per kilobase per million mapped reads) for every gene in the four different tissues analyzed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 18</a>). Differentially expressed genes were detected using the method described by Chen <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Chen, S. et al. De novo analysis of transcriptome dynamics in the migratory locust during the development of phase traits. PLoS ONE 5, e15633 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1475">16</a></sup>. Using false discovery rate (FDR) <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05 as the threshold for significance, we found 2,283, 1,499, 947 and 1,288 genes that were preferentially expressed in flower, leaf, stem and root, respectively. Among the genes preferentially expressed in flowers, there was high enrichment for GO terms related to 'cell wall', 'cell wall modification' and 'pectinesterase activity' (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM19">Supplementary Table 19</a>), confirming the correlation of modification and organization of cell walls with flower development and wilting<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="O'Donoghue, E.M., Somerfield, S.D. &amp; Heyes, J.A. Organization of cell walls in Sandersonia aurantiaca floral tissue. J. Exp. Bot. 53, 513â523 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1489">17</a></sup>. In leaves, GO terms related to 'photosynthesis', 'electron transport chain' and 'photosystem' were significantly enriched (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05), consistent with photosynthesis being the major function of this tissue. The 'heme binding' and 'iron ion binding' functions were enriched in both root and stem, suggesting that both organs have an important role in metal ion metabolism in orchid. GO terms related to 'transition metal ion metabolism' were also uncovered in GO term enrichment analysis for gene families with <i>P. equestris</i>âspecific expansion and might suggest that the metabolism of transition metals (including iron, zinc and copper) in both stems and roots has an important role in adaptation to <i>P. equestris</i>âspecific epiphytic growth niches. Mineral ions in epiphytic growth niches are unevenly distributed, usually diluted and only sporadically available in comparison to their distribution in growth niches in soils<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="LÃ¼ttge, U. Vascular Plants as Epiphytes: Evolution and Ecophysiology (Springer-Verlag, 1989)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1502">18</a></sup>. Gene duplication in these metabolic pathways might have contributed to different regulation of mineral ion metabolism adapted to epiphytic niches. It would be very interesting to see whether the same gene families have also been expanded in other non-orchid epiphytes (such as bromeliads).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Heterozygosity</h3><p>To estimate the level of heterozygosity in the genome, we carried out <i>k</i>-mer distribution approximation with simulated heterozygous genome sequences and found that the real <i>k</i>-mer distribution was fitted best by a simulated <i>k</i>-mer (<i>k</i> represents the chosen length of substrings) distribution with 1.2% (between 1.1% and 1.3%) heterozygosity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). We also investigated the level of heterozygosity in the assembled part of the genome by mapping all reads back to the assembly, finding that the heterozygosity was about 0.4%. Because the part of the genome showing the lowest heterozygosity was the best assembled part, 0.4% is probably an underestimation due to sampling bias.</p><p>With a heterozygous genome, the assembler might assemble the two alleles for a site separately, which would result in an excess of the assembly with half of the average sequencing depth. To plot the sequencing depth distribution, we mapped all the sequencing reads to the assembly; we found that, although the major peak of sequencing depth was at <span class="stix">â¼</span>100Ã, there was indeed a minor peak at <span class="stix">â¼</span>50Ã (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>), indicating that there were genomic regions with half of the average sequencing depth due to underassembly of allelic regions with high heterozygosity. Using the depth distribution, we estimated the length of the genome assembly from these heterozygous regions to be 135 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). We then identified 58,241 contigs with half of the average sequencing depth (total length of 131.2 Mb, consistent with the estimation of heterozygous region length) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>); these contigs explained the 50Ã peak for sequencing depth, as the depth distribution was normal after excluding them (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>). The 2,454 genes from these contigs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM20">Supplementary Table 20</a>) were significantly over-represented in the GO terms 'apoptosis', (<i>P</i> value 4.19 Ã 10<sup>â6</sup>) 'programmed cell death' (<i>P</i> value 4.19 Ã 10<sup>â6</sup>) and 'defense response' (<i>P</i> value 1.77 Ã 10<sup>â4</sup>) and are possibly related to self-incompatibility<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Bosch, M., Poulter, N.S., Vatovec, S. &amp; Franklin-Tong, V.E. Initiation of programmed cell death in self-incompatibility: role for cytoskeleton modifications and several caspase-like activities. Mol. Plant 1, 879â887 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1567">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Dixit, R. &amp; Nasrallah, J.B. Recognizing self in the self-incompatibility response. Plant Physiol. 125, 105â108 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1570">20</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 21</a>). The heterozygous contigs suggest that there is a block-wise distribution of heterozygosity, and we further identified heterozygous SNPs in the genome to characterize their distribution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). We indeed found that the 1.7 million high-quality heterozygous SNPs identified were not distributed randomly in the genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">TE insertions in introns</h3><p>We compared the gene models in 13 plant species and found that the average intron length for <i>P. equestris</i> was substantially longer than for the other species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#Tab1">Table 1</a>). In comparing the distributions of TE proportions in introns, we identified a distinctive major peak near 45% in orchid (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 22</a>). In addition, the proportion of genes with long introns seemed substantially higher in <i>P. equestris</i> than in most other species, even after excluding TEs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). Further comparison of intron length distributions showed that <i>P. equestris</i> had the highest proportion of introns with a length of â¥2,000 bp (27.7%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 23</a>). To explore the functional consequences of intronic TE insertions, we compared the expression levels of genes with TE insertions to those of corresponding paralogous genes without intronic TE insertions. Overall, the expression levels of genes with TE insertions were lower than those of their paralogs (<i>P</i> &lt; 1 Ã 10<sup>â11</sup>, Wilcoxon rank-sum paired test) in all four tissues examined (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>). A decrease in the expression levels of these genes can probably be explained by negative selection due to an increase in the transcription cost for the longer transcript, which is consistent with previous findings suggesting that natural selection favors short introns in highly expressed genes to minimize the cost of transcription and other molecular processes such as splicing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Castillo-Davis, C.I., Mekhedov, S.L., Hartl, D.L., Koonin, E.V. &amp; Kondrashov, F.A. Selection for short introns in highly expressed genes. Nat. Genet. 31, 415â418 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1625">21</a></sup>. Previous studies on paralogs formed by whole-genome duplication (WGD) showed significant differences in expression levels, with genes with weaker expression having a tendency to accumulate more transposon insertions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Schnable, J.C., Springer, N.M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069â4074 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d46199e1629">22</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Comparison of the gene models in different species</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.3149/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome evolution</h3><p>We constructed a phylogenetic tree on the basis of a concatenated sequence alignment of the 72 single-copy genes shared by orchid and 10 other green plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). In this phylogenetic tree, orchid, as expected, clustered with other monocots, although the evolutionary distance from orchid to cereals such as rice, <i>Brachypodium</i> species, sorghum and maize was relatively large. Although the 72 genes already provided enough phylogenetic signals for phylogeny construction in the 11 green plant species, accurate dating of the divergence times between orchid and other monocots requires a larger gene set while compromising the phylogenetic coverage. Applying the PAML MCMCTree program to the 342 single-copy genes shared among orchid and 7 other species (monocots and dicots), the divergence time between <i>P. equestris</i> and the other monocots was estimated at 135.1 Â± 17 million years ago, which is in line with estimates from both angiosperm-wide<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Bell, C.D., Soltis, D.E. &amp; Soltis, P.S. The age and diversification of the angiosperms re-revisited. Am. J. Bot. 97, 1296â1303 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2653">23</a></sup> and Orchidaceae-specific<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="RamÃ­rez, S.R., Gravendeel, B., Singer, R.B., Marshall, C.R. &amp; Pierce, N.E. Dating the origin of the Orchidaceae from a fossil orchid with its pollinator. Nature 448, 1042â1045 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2657">13</a></sup> molecular sequence divergence estimation studies. We also studied gene family expansion and contraction in different evolutionary lineages (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>).</p><p>Like many other plant genomes sequenced thus far<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Cui, L. et al. Widespread genome duplications throughout the history of flowering plants. Genome Res. 16, 738â749 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2668">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Proost, S., Pattyn, P., Gerats, T. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Journey through the past: 150 million years of plant genome evolution. Plant J. 66, 58â65 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2671">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Soltis, D.E. et al. Polyploidy and angiosperm diversification. Am. J. Bot. 96, 336â348 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2674">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous-Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334â1347 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2677">27</a></sup>, the orchid genome harbored the remnants of one or more large-scale duplication events. Although only a small fraction of the genome (3.51%) showed collinearity (conservation of gene order and content) with other regions in the genome, this proportion most likely constitutes a substantial underestimate. Indeed, in total, 12,000 orchid genes resided on scaffolds with fewer than 20 genes, which are of limited use for the intragenome detection of collinearity. Furthermore, about 6,500 genes were located on scaffolds with fewer than 5 genes. However, a considerable number of genes (5,492) were contained in syntenic regions that showed conservation of gene content, regardless of gene order. The notable difference between retained homeologs in collinear versus syntenic regions can probably be explained by a high degree of reshuffling of genes after duplication, fractionation (loss of either homeolog) and the low gene density of <i>P. equestris</i> (which has about the same number of genes as <i>A. thaliana</i> with a genome size that is about ten times larger). In the absence of a close outgroup and because other sequenced monocot species diverged more than 100 million years ago, the above factors render multi-level collinearity with other sequenced angiosperm species hard to detect. However, analysis of the number of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (<i>K</i><sub>S</sub>) for the whole paranome (the set of all duplicated genes in the genome), identified a peak between 0.6 and 1.1 that corresponds to contemporarily created gene duplicates (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 13a</a>) and most likely represents an ancient WGD event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Vanneste, K., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Maere, S. Inference of genome duplications from age distributions revisited. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 177â190 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2695">28</a></sup>. Furthermore, when only duplicates retained in collinear regions were considered, duplicates from small-scale duplications were excluded from the distribution and the WGD signature peak in the <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distribution became even more pronounced (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 13b</a>). Putative peaks at higher <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values might represent more ancient WGD events in the monocot lineage that might have been shared by orchids or their ancestors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Kim, C. et al. Comparative analysis of Miscanthus and Saccharum reveals a shared whole-genome duplication but different evolutionary fates. Plant Cell 26, 2420â2429 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2710">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Jiao, Y., Li, J., Tang, H. &amp; Paterson, A.H. Integrated syntenic and phylogenomic analyses reveal an ancient genome duplication in monocots. Plant Cell 26, 2792â2802 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2713">30</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 13b</a>).</p><p>We performed absolute dating through phylogenomic analysis to establish the age of this paleopolyploidy in relation to the monocot phylogeny<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous-Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334â1347 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2724">27</a></sup>. The absolute dating of genes present under the signature WGD peak in the <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distribution rendered an absolute age distribution with a clear peak at <span class="stix">â¼</span>76 million years ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). More specifically, the WGD event was dated at 75.57 million years ago, with lower and upper 90% confidence interval limits of 71.50 and 80.73 million years ago, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>). As the common ancestor of the crown group of orchids is supposed to have lived during the Late Cretaceous period sometime between 76 and 84 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="RamÃ­rez, S.R., Gravendeel, B., Singer, R.B., Marshall, C.R. &amp; Pierce, N.E. Dating the origin of the Orchidaceae from a fossil orchid with its pollinator. Nature 448, 1042â1045 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2738">13</a></sup>, this finding would suggest that the orchid-specific WGD event occurred in association with the origin of this clade, and polyploidy is indeed proposed as a frequent mechanism of speciation in angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Wood, T.E. et al. The frequency of polyploid speciation in vascular plants. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 13875â13879 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2743">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Soltis, D.E., Visger, C.J. &amp; Soltis, P.S. The polyploidy revolution thenâ¦and now: Stebbins revisited. Am. J. Bot. 101, 1057â1078 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2746">32</a></sup>. In contrast, many members of the Orchidaceae family underwent drastic rate shifts (transition and transversion) during their evolutionary history due to periods of accelerated molecular evolution caused by their short life cycles and altered life history strategies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Whitten, W.M. et al. Molecular phylogenetics of Maxillaria and related genera (Orchidaceae: Cymbidieae) based on combined molecular data sets. Am. J. Bot. 94, 1860â1889 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2750">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Whitten, W.M., Williams, N.H. &amp; Chase, M.W. Subtribal and generic relationships of Maxillarieae (Orchidaceae) with emphasis on Stanhopeinae: combined molecular evidence. Am. J. Bot. 87, 1842â1856 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2753">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Douzery, E.J. et al. Molecular phylogenetics of diseae (Orchidaceae): a contribution from nuclear ribosomal ITS sequences. Am. J. Bot. 86, 887â899 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2756">35</a></sup>. These rate shifts complicate absolute dating<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous-Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334â1347 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2760">27</a></sup>, especially considering the long distant relationship from orchid to the other monocot species for which the complete genome sequence is currently available, such that the current WGD age estimate could be an overestimate, with the actual age most likely closer to the lower confidence interval boundary. This WGD age estimate would suggest that paleopolyploidy enabled survival across the Cretaceous-Paleogene boundary, as witnessed in many other angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Vanneste, K., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Tangled up in two: a burst of genome duplications at the end of the Cretaceous and the consequences for plant evolution. Phil. Trans. R. Soc. Lond. B 369, 20130353 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2764">36</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). Determining whether orchid-specific paleopolyploidy occurred in association with either its origin or the Cretaceous-Paleogene boundary will necessitate information on other non-cereal monocot genomes. Nevertheless, the orchid-specific paleopolyploidy identified in this study followed by the documented vast radiation of orchid not long after the Cretaceous-Paleogene boundary<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="RamÃ­rez, S.R., Gravendeel, B., Singer, R.B., Marshall, C.R. &amp; Pierce, N.E. Dating the origin of the Orchidaceae from a fossil orchid with its pollinator. Nature 448, 1042â1045 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2771">13</a></sup>, which enabled Orchidaceae to become the second largest angiosperm plant family with a remarkable diversity in flower morphology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Campbell, C.S., Judd, W.S. &amp; Kellogg, E.A. Plant Systematics: A Phylogenetic Approach (Sinauer Associates, 1999)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2776">37</a></sup>, might suggest that the WGD event contributed to the success of orchids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Van de Peer, Y., Maere, S. &amp; Meyer, A. The evolutionary significance of ancient genome duplications. Nat. Rev. Genet. 10, 725â732 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2780">38</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Dating the paleopolyploidy event in P. equestris."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Dating the paleopolyploidy event in <i>P. equestris</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3149/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3149/MediaObjects/41588_2015_Article_BFng3149_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3149/MediaObjects/41588_2015_Article_BFng3149_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="424"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Absolute age distribution obtained by phylogenomic dating of <i>P. equestris</i> homeologs. The solid black line represents the kernel density estimate of the dated homeologs, and the vertical dashed black line represents its peak, used as the WGD age estimate. Gray lines represent the density estimates for the 1,000 bootstrap replicates, and the vertical black dotted lines represent the corresponding 90% confidence intervals for the WGD age estimate. The original raw distribution of dated homeologs is also indicated by dots. The mode used as an estimate for the consensus WGD age is found at 75.57 million years ago with lower and upper 90% confidence interval boundaries at 71.50 and 80.73 million years ago, respectively <i>y</i> axis represents percentage of gene pairs. (<b>b</b>) Phylogenetic tree of the angiosperms. A wave of WGD events (indicated by colored bars) appears to be associated with the Cretaceous-Paleogene extinction event <span class="stix">â¼</span>66 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous-Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334â1347 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2812">27</a></sup>. The orchid-specific WGD is indicated by the unfilled bar.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3149/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Evolutionary analysis of CAM genes</h3><p>In contrast to all vascular plants, of which about 10% are estimated to be epiphytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="LÃ¼ttge, U. Ecophysiology of crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM). Ann. Bot. 93, 629â652 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2832">39</a></sup>, most orchid species (72%) are epiphytes, with the majority being restricted to tropical regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Benzing, D.H. Vascular epiphytism: taxonomic participation and adaptive diversity. Ann. Mo. Bot. Gard. 74, 183â204 (1987)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2836">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Gravendeel, B., Smithson, A., Slik, F.J. &amp; Schuiteman, A. Epiphytism and pollinator specialization: drivers for orchid diversity? Phil. Trans. R. Soc. Lond. B 359, 1523â1535 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2839">41</a></sup>. Many orchids use the CAM pathway for photosynthesis rather than the C<sub>3</sub> pathway, which is considered to be an adaptation to their epiphytic lifestyle that limits water supply. CAM is an important metabolic pathway that evolved convergently in many different plant lineages, and it has been estimated that CAM pathway components are encoded by at least 343 genera in 35 plant families, comprising <span class="stix">â¼</span>6% of flowering plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="PospiÅ¡ilovÃ¡, J. Vascular plants as epiphytes. Evolution and ecophysiology. Biol. Plant. 33, 500 (1991)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2845">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Holtum, J.A., Winter, K., Weeks, M.A. &amp; Sexton, T.R. Crassulacean acid metabolism in the ZZ plant, Zamioculcas zamiifolia (Araceae). Am. J. Bot. 94, 1670â1676 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2848">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Silvera, K. et al. Evolution along the crassulacean acid metabolism continuum. Funct. Plant Biol. 37, 995â1010 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d46199e2851">44</a></sup>. The CAM pathway bears resemblance to the C<sub>4</sub> pathway in that both act to concentrate CO<sub>2</sub> around RuBisCO, thereby increasing its efficiency. However, where C<sub>4</sub> plants concentrate CO<sub>2</sub> spatially, CAM plants concentrate CO<sub>2</sub> temporally, providing CO<sub>2</sub> during the day but not at night when respiration is the dominant reaction. <i>P. equestris</i> is the only CAM plant thus far for which the genome has been sequenced.</p><p>To gain further insight into the evolution of the CAM pathway from the ancestral C<sub>3</sub> pathway, we identified genes encoding the key enzymes of the CAM pathway (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#Fig3">Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 24</a>) and compared the <i>P. equestris</i> CAM genes with their homologs in Poaceae, including <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i> and <i>Z. mays</i>, using the dicot <i>A. thaliana</i> as an outgroup. We analyzed six key enzymes in CAM, namely, carbonic anhydrase (<i>CA</i>), malic enzyme (<i>ME</i>), malate dehydrogenase (<i>MDH</i>), pyruvate phosphate dikinase (<i>PPDK</i>), phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase (<i>PPCK</i>) and phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase. Gene trees were constructed from alignments of the coding sequences for each gene family (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 14</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Data Set</a>). We identified gene duplication and loss events along the lineage leading to <i>P. equestris</i> by manually inspecting each gene tree individually. In particular, we uncovered one gene loss and one gene duplication event in the <i>PEPC</i> gene family, two gene duplications in the <i>ME</i> gene family (in the <i>NADP-ME</i> subfamily), one gene loss in the <i>PPCK</i> gene family and at least six gene duplications in the <i>CA</i> gene family (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). <i>CA</i> catalyzes the reaction converting carbon dioxide into carbonate, which is the first step in CO<sub>2</sub> fixation. There are two <i>CA</i> subfamilies (Î± and Î²) in <i>P. equestris</i>. The most obvious expansion for a gene family was found in the Î± <i>CA</i> gene family. However, the functional differentiation between the two gene families is still not clear. Gene family expansion might substantially increase the efficiency of the reaction through dosage effects and might also provide the possibility of adaptive evolution of the duplicated copies. We did not find any CAM genes among the retained duplicates in the WGD-derived homeologous segments and therefore did not find any obvious evidence that the paleopolyploidy event has been of crucial importance for CAM evolution in <i>P. equestris</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Overview of crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) pathway evolution."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Overview of crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) pathway evolution.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3149/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3149/MediaObjects/41588_2015_Article_BFng3149_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3149/MediaObjects/41588_2015_Article_BFng3149_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="980"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Overview of the CAM pathway with components for which the respective gene family underwent gene duplication or loss as indicated. A star indicates components whose gene families underwent gene loss or gain. CA, carbonic anhydrase; CC, Calvin cycle; PEP, phosphoenolpyruvic acid; PEPC, phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase; PPCK, phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase; MDH, malate dehydrogenase; ME, malic enzyme; PPDK, pyruvate phosphate dikinase.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3149/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">MADS-box gene family analysis</h3><p>MADS-box genes are known to be involved in many important processes during plant development but are especially known for their roles in flower development. Because orchids are famous for their flower morphology, we focused on identifying and characterizing the MADS-box genes in more detail. In total, 51 putative functional MADS-box genes and 9 pseudogenes were identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#Tab2">Table 2</a>). Perhaps surprisingly, these numbers are smaller than what is documented for most other sequenced angiosperms. <i>P. equestris</i> has 29 type II MADS-box genes, much fewer than the number found in rice (48) or other cereals. Phylogenetic analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>) showed that most of the genes in the type II MADS-box clades had been duplicated, except those in the B-PI, Bs, SVP and MIKC* clades. Among the duplicated type II clades, the E-class (six members), C/D-class (five members, three in C class and two in D class), B-class <i>AP3</i> (four members) and <i>AGL6</i> clades (three members) contained more genes than <i>A. thaliana</i> and rice (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 25</a>). However, genes from the <i>FLC</i>, <i>AGL12</i> and <i>AGL15</i> clades could not be found in the <i>P. equestris</i> genome. Recently, <i>FLC</i> genes have been found in cereals, but they are difficult to identify because they are highly divergent and relatively short<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Ruelens, P. et al. FLOWERING LOCUS C in monocots and the tandem origin of angiosperm-specific MADS-box genes. Nat. Commun. 4, 2280 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3025">45</a></sup>. However, genes in both the <i>AGL12</i> and <i>AGL15</i> clades are present in the genomes of rice and <i>A. thaliana</i>; therefore, orthologs of <i>FLC</i>, <i>AGL12</i> and <i>AGL15</i> might have been specifically lost in orchids. Although <i>AGL12</i>-like genes (<i>XAL1</i> in <i>A. thaliana</i>) are necessary for root development and flowering<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Tapia-LÃ³pez, R. et al. An AGAMOUS-related MADS-box gene, XAL1 (AGL12), regulates root meristem cell proliferation and flowering transition in Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 146, 1182â1192 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3057">46</a></sup>, it seems that a different mechanism has evolved in <i>P. equestris</i> for the same function. Genes in the B-class <i>AP3</i>, C/D-class and E-class clades are well known for their roles in the specification of floral organ identity and have been well studied in <i>P. equestris</i>. These expanded clades including members with differential expression patterns in orchid floral organs, as well as divergent encoded protein domains, support the unique evolutionary routes of these floral organ identity genes associated with the unique labellum and gynostemium innovation in orchids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Pan, Z.J. et al. The duplicated B-class MADS-box genes display dualistic characters in orchid floral organ identity and growth. Plant Cell Physiol. 52, 1515â1531 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3071">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Tsai, W.C. et al. Interactions of B-class complex proteins involved in tepal development in Phalaenopsis orchid. Plant Cell Physiol. 49, 814â824 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3074">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Tsai, W.C., Kuoh, C.S., Chuang, M.H., Chen, W.H. &amp; Chen, H.H. Four DEF-like MADS box genes displayed distinct floral morphogenetic roles in Phalaenopsis orchid. Plant Cell Physiol. 45, 831â844 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3077">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Pan, Z.J. et al. Flower development of Phalaenopsis orchid involves functionally divergent SEPALLATA-like genes. New Phytol. 202, 1024â1042 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3080">50</a></sup>. Notably, one of the three gene copies in the expanded <i>AGL6</i> clade had an expression pattern similar to that of the <i>AP3</i>-like <i>PeMADS4</i> gene, which is specifically expressed in the labellum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Hsiao, Y.Y. et al. Transcriptomic analysis of floral organs from Phalaenopsis orchid by using oligonucleotide microarray. Gene 518, 91â100 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3094">51</a></sup>. The <i>AGL6</i>-like gene <i>OsMADS6</i> could specify floral organ identities and meristem fate by interacting with the floral homeotic genes <i>SUPERWOMAN1</i>, <i>MADS3</i>, <i>MADS58</i>, <i>MADS13</i> and <i>DROOPING LEAF</i> in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97â100 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3120">52</a></sup>. The <i>OsMADS6</i> gene product has also been shown to act as an integrator by forming complexes with B-class and C/D-class MADS-box proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Seok, H.Y. et al. Rice ternary MADS protein complexes containing class B MADS heterodimer. Biochem. Biophys. Res. Commun. 401, 598â604 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3127">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Favaro, R. et al. Ovule-specific MADS-box proteins have conserved protein-protein interactions in monocot and dicot plants. Mol. Genet. Genomics 268, 152â159 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3130">54</a></sup>. Combinatorial protein interaction networks among the members of the expanded B-class, C/D-class, E-class and <i>AGL6</i> clades during orchid floral development might have led to the evolutionary novelties of orchid flowers directly involved in speciation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Pan, Z.J. et al. Flower development of Phalaenopsis orchid involves functionally divergent SEPALLATA-like genes. New Phytol. 202, 1024â1042 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3138">50</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 MADS-box genes in the <i>P. equestris</i>, poplar, <i>A. thaliana</i> and rice genomes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.3149/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Only 22 putative functional type I genes and 8 pseudogenes were found (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#Tab2">Table 2</a>), suggesting that the <i>P. equestris</i> type I MADS-box genes have experienced a lower birth rate or, alternatively, a higher death rate than type II MADS-box genes. Tandem gene duplications seem to have contributed to the increase in the number of type I genes in the Î± group (type I MÎ±) and suggest that the type I MADS-box genes have mainly been duplicated by smaller-scale and more recent duplications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="ParenicovÃ¡, L. et al. Molecular and phylogenetic analyses of the complete MADS-box transcription factor family in Arabidopsis: new openings to the MADS world. Plant Cell 15, 1538â1551 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3746">55</a></sup>. Interestingly, the <i>P. equestris</i> genome does not contain the Î² group of type I MADS-box genes (type I MÎ²), although these do exist in <i>A. thaliana</i>, poplar and rice. Interactions among type I MADS-box genes are important for the initiation of endosperm development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Masiero, S., Colombo, L., Grini, P.E., Schnittger, A. &amp; Kater, M.M. The emerging importance of type I MADS box transcription factors for plant reproduction. Plant Cell 23, 865â872 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3757">56</a></sup>.</p><p>We also determined the expression levels of all orchid MADS-box genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 26</a>) and found that 20 of these genes were preferentially expressed in flower tissue. In particular, five genes (<i>PEQU_41930</i> (D), <i>PEQU_16438</i> (D), <i>PEQU_12328</i> (Bs), <i>PEQU_17261</i> (MÎ±) and <i>PEQU_09539</i> (MIKC*)) were exclusively expressed in flower, suggestive of a distinct role in orchid floral morphogenesis.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Here we have presented a high-quality draft sequence of <i>P. equestris</i>, the first orchid for which the genome sequence has been determined. All around the world, orchids are highly endangered species because of illegal collection and loss of habitat. The complete genome sequence of <i>P. equestris</i> will provide an important resource to start exploring orchid diversity and evolution at the genome level, which will be important for ecological and conservation purposes. The genome sequence will also be a key resource for the development of new concepts and techniques in genetic engineering, such as molecular markerâassisted breeding and the production of transgenic plants, which are necessary to increase the efficiency of orchid breeding and aid orchid horticulture research.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Sample preparation and sequencing.</h3><p>For genome sequencing, we collected leaves and flowers from several individuals of an inbred line of <i>P. equestris</i> and extracted genomic DNA using the modified CTAB protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Murray, M.G. &amp; Thompson, W.F. Rapid isolation of high molecular weight plant DNA. Nucleic Acids Res. 8, 4321â4325 (1980)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3813">58</a></sup>. Sequencing libraries with insert sizes ranging from 160 bp to 20 kb were then constructed using a library construction kit (Illumina). Libraries were sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform. A library with an insert size of 40 kb was constructed using a modified fosmid library construction pipeline as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Zhang, G. et al. Genome sequence of foxtail millet (Setaria italica) provides insights into grass evolution and biofuel potential. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 549â554 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3817">59</a></sup>. The raw reads generated were filtered according to sequencing quality and with regard to adaptor contamination and duplicated reads. Thus, only high-quality reads were remained and used in the genome assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome assembly and assessment.</h3><p>We adopted a whole-genome shotgun strategy to sequence and assemble the genome of <i>P. equestris</i> and obtained 119.4 Gb of data from seven DNA libraries (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 1</a>). Using <i>k</i>-mer frequency distribution analysis, we estimated the genome size to be 1.16 Gb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>), which is smaller than previous estimates by flow cytometry (<span class="stix">â¼</span>1.6 Gb; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Lin, S. et al. Nuclear DNA contents of Phalaenopsis sp. and Doritis pulcherrima. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 126, 195â199 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3844">5</a>). In the <i>k</i>-mer distribution, we also observed a secondary peak indicating considerable heterozygosity, which posed serious challenges for the genome assembly algorithm.
