In general, genome assembly was carried out using SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265â272 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3851">60</a></sup>. Contig construction, scaffolding and gap filling processes were performed using the corresponding methods provided by SOAPdenovo. The parameters used in genome assembly were as follows: pregraph -s Pha.lib -a 200 -p 12 -K 35 -d 2 -o Pha; contig -g Pha_1213 -M 3; map -s Pha.lib -g Pha; scaff -g Pha. Using these data, we assembled 236,185 scaffolds, with a total length of <span class="stix">â¼</span>1.1 Gb and an N50 length of 359.1 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 2</a>). About 90% of the total assembled genome (<span class="stix">â¼</span>980 Mb) was contained in the 6,359 longest scaffolds.</p><p>The total genome assembly amounted to 1.086 Gb (1.0 Gb without N bases), which is <span class="stix">â¼</span>93% (<span class="stix">â¼</span>86% without N bases) of the estimated total genome size. We assessed the coverage of the genome assembly using BACs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>). First, 18,486 BAC-end sequences (&gt;100 bp in length) from a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Hsu, C.-C. et al. An overview of the Phalaenopsis orchid genome through BAC end sequence analysis. BMC Plant Biol. 11, 3 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3864">61</a></sup> and our data (W.-C. Tsai and H.-H. Chen, unpublished data) were downloaded and mapped back to the scaffolds using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Kent, W.J. BLATâthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3868">62</a></sup>. Of the BAC-end sequences, 92.9% could be mapped to the assembled genome. We then used a pooling strategy to sequence the BAC clones from a BAC library of <i>P. equestris</i>. We made mixtures of 10, 20 and 30 randomly selected BAC clones, each with an independent replication. Each of the six pooled BAC clones was amplified with liquid medium and then used to extract BAC DNA. The pooled BAC DNA was sheared to generate sequencing libraries of short insert size, and these libraries were sequenced. For each BAC pool, we obtained more than 5 Gb of data on average. Taking these data, we used SOAPdenovo to assemble each BAC pool. Although the overall assembly results of these BACs had some gaps (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 27</a>), we were able to generate some long contigs and use these to assess the quality of the whole-genome assembly. We mapped these long contigs back to the genome assembly with BLASTZ. The mapped length was then calculated, and the mapping details were displayed. For contigs longer than 1 kb in length (979 in total), 97% could be mapped to the assembled genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). Finally, we sequenced and assembled ten randomly chosen BAC clones using 454 sequencing technology. Comparison of these assembled BAC scaffolds with our assembly also indicated a low error rate (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>We also assessed the completeness and accuracy of the assembly using conserved genes and RNA-seq data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>). Of the 248 conserved core eukaryotic genes that were used to assess genome completeness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Leitch, I.J. et al. Genome size diversity in orchids: consequences and evolution. Ann. Bot. 104, 469â481 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3891">6</a></sup>, 234 (94%) were uncovered in our genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 5</a>). Using the <span class="stix">â¼</span>9 Gb of RNA-seq data from four different <i>P. equestris</i> tissues (leaf, flower, stem and root), we assembled transcription fragments, and 93% (root) and <span class="stix">â¼</span>97% (leaf) of the assembled sequences could be mapped to the genome assembly with 90% identity and 90% mapping coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Table 6</a>). Thus, the coverage of the assembly in gene-rich regions was estimated to be &gt; 93%, which was higher than the estimated genome coverage overall.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Repeat annotation.</h3><p>Tandem repeats and TEs in the genome were identified separately. The repeat annotation process was similar to that applied and described in a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Zhang, G. et al. Genome sequence of foxtail millet (Setaria italica) provides insights into grass evolution and biofuel potential. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 549â554 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3912">59</a></sup>. Tandem repeats were identified using TRF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3916">63</a></sup> and RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics Chapter 4, Unit 4.10 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3920">64</a></sup> (version 3.2.7). To identify TEs, we first used RepeatMasker with the Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3924">65</a></sup> database of known repeat sequences to search the TEs in the orchid genome. We then used LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3928">66</a></sup> (version 1.0.3), PILER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Edgar, R.C. &amp; Myers, E.W. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21 (suppl. 1), i152âi158 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3933">67</a></sup> and RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Price, A.L., Jones, N.C. &amp; Pevzner, P.A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21 (suppl. 1), i351âi358 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3937">68</a></sup> (version 1.05) to construct a repeat sequence database for orchid. Further, applying this <i>de novo</i> repeat sequence database, we used RepeatMasker to search for repeats in the genome. We also used RepeatProteinMask (version 3.2.2) implemented in RepeatMasker to identify repeat proteins. All the repeat sequences identified by the different methods were combined into the final repeat annotation. The repeat elements were categorized in a hierarchical way, as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Zhang, G. et al. Genome sequence of foxtail millet (Setaria italica) provides insights into grass evolution and biofuel potential. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 549â554 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3944">59</a></sup>.</p><p>To study the divergence of LTRs, we identified LTRs with complete structure using LTR_STRUC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="McCarthy, E.M. &amp; McDonald, J.F. LTR_STRUC: a novel search and identification program for LTR retrotransposons. Bioinformatics 19, 362â367 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3951">69</a></sup> with default parameters. Divergence was then estimated as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Zhang, G. et al. Genome sequence of foxtail millet (Setaria italica) provides insights into grass evolution and biofuel potential. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 549â554 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3955">59</a></sup>. The LTRs with complete structure were aligned using MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Edgar, R.C. MUSCLE: a multiple sequence alignment method with reduced time and space complexity. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 113 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3959">70</a></sup>, and divergence was estimated using the Kimura two-parameter model in distmat implemented in the EMBOSS package.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Gene annotation.</h3><p>Gene annotation was performed as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Zhang, G. et al. Genome sequence of foxtail millet (Setaria italica) provides insights into grass evolution and biofuel potential. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 549â554 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3971">59</a></sup>. We carried out gene annotation using the following methods: (i) <i>de novo</i> gene prediction, (ii) homolog prediction, (iii) RNA-seq annotation and (iv) integration of the gene set. First, two <i>de novo</i> gene prediction programs, AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435âW439 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3981">71</a></sup> (version 2.03) and SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d46199e3985">72</a></sup>, were applied to predict genes on the masked genome sequences. In homolog prediction, we utilized the protein sequences from four angiosperm genomes (<i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i> and <i>Z. mays</i>) to align against the unmasked genome using TBLASTN, with an <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 1 Ã 10<sup>â5</sup>, and then used Genewise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988â995 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4008">73</a></sup> (version 2.2.0) to predict gene structures. In RNA-seq annotation, the RNA-seq reads from four tissues were aligned against the reference genome using TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105â1111 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4012">74</a></sup> (version 1.0.14). After alignment, the transcripts were assembled using Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511â515 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4016">75</a></sup> (version 0.8.2). BESTORF was then used to predict ORFs with parameters trained on monocot genes without filtering out UTRs. Finally, we generated an integrated gene set using GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Elsik, C.G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4020">76</a></sup>. Details on software parameters are provided in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#MOESM14">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><p>To detect alternative splicing, we first used an in-house script to train fifth-order Markov parameters with our annotated gene set. Using this training set, we predicted an ORF for each transcript generated by Cufflinks. Transcripts without predicted ORFs were discarded. The transcripts predicted were compared to our gene models. Redundant transcripts, transcripts encoding short proteins (&lt;50 amino acids in length) and transcripts for which the protein product was shorter than 30% of the proteins encoded in the gene set were filtered out.</p><p>For gene model validation, we manually examined 500 randomly selected genes from 2,038 of the gene families shared among monocots that only had one copy in both <i>P. equestris</i> and rice. Most of these 500 genes were correctly predicted, although 20 contained dubious annotation errors, reflecting an annotation accuracy of 96%. We further manually checked the well-studied MADS-box genes in <i>P. equestris</i> and found that the annotation of those genes was consistent with previous gene models determined by the sequencing of full-length cDNAs (W.-C.T., Y.-Y.H., K.-W.L., Z.-J.L., G.-Q.Z. <i>et al</i>., unpublished data). Of 40 genes, only 3 had incomplete annotations, again reflecting the high quality of the gene annotation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Building gene families.</h3><p>To build gene families, the PLAZA pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Proost, S. et al. PLAZA: a comparative genomics resource to study gene and genome evolution in plants. Plant Cell 21, 3718â3731 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4051">15</a></sup> was used. Along with the orchid genome, we included the genomes of four monocots (<i>O. sativa</i>, <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i> and <i>Z. mays</i>), three eudicots (<i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>) and three outgroup species (<i>P. patens</i>, <i>C. reinhardtii</i> and <i>O. lucimarinus</i>). First, all pairwise similarities between the 364,344 coding genes in the data set were calculated using all-against-all BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Altschul, S.F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389â3402 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4087">77</a></sup>, retaining the top 1,250 hits for each gene with an <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 1 Ã 10<sup>â5</sup>. Using tribeMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Enright, A.J., Van Dongen, S. &amp; Ouzounis, C.A. An efficient algorithm for large-scale detection of protein families. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 1575â1584 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4096">78</a></sup>, these similarities were clustered into homologous gene families (in mclblastline, using <i>I</i> = 2 and scheme = 4; other parameters were left at default values).</p><p>Genes included in a family with a similarity score (BLASTP) of less than 25% of the median similarity score for gene pairs within that family were flagged as outliers and were not included in the alignment and phylogenetic tree. For each family, the amino acid sequences encoded by all genes were obtained and aligned using MUSCLE. Ambiguously aligned positions (sites with gaps in the majority of the sequences and misalignments) were automatically removed from the alignments. Note that each singleton, for example, a gene with no homologs, was considered to represent a separate gene family (as shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3149#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Functional annotation.</h3><p>Two methods were used for functional annotation. First, InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Hunter, S. et al. InterPro: the integrative protein signature database. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D211âD215 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4117">14</a></sup> (using default settings) was run to map known protein domains to all genes. Using InterPro to GO mapping, GO labels were obtained for the InterPro domains. Second, on the basis of phylogenetic trees, reliable rice orthologs were identified for orchid genes. Functional annotations from the orthologous rice genes, excluding those with an evidence tag of 'inferred from electronic annotation', were transferred to the orchid genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Detection of genomic homology.</h3><p>Genomic homology was detected using i-ADHoRe 3.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Proost, S. et al. i-ADHoRe 3.0âfast and sensitive detection of genomic homology in extremely large data sets. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e11 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4128">79</a>), included in the PLAZA pipeline, using the following settings: alignment method gg2, gap size 30, tandem gap 30, cluster gap 35, q value 0.85, prob cutoff 0.01, anchor points 5 and multiple hypothesis correction FDR). The output was processed by the pipeline and included in a relational database to which visualization programs can connect and on which additional statistical analysis can be performed. For synteny detection, the cloud mode was enabled (cluster_type = cloud) and appropriate settings were selected: cloud_gap_size 20, cloud_cluster_gap 20, cloud_filter_method binomial, prob cutoff 0.01, anchor points 5, multiple hypothesis correction FDR and level_2_only true.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Relative dating using synonymous substitutions.</h3><p><i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values for homologous gene pairs were calculated by first aligning the coding sequences with ClustalW<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Thompson, J.D., Gibson, T.J. &amp; Higgins, D.G. Multiple sequence alignment using ClustalW and ClustalX. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics Chapter 2, Unit 2.3 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4143">80</a></sup> using the protein sequences as a guide. Positions aligned with low confidence (regions near gaps in the alignment) were stripped. Codeml (PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Yang, Z. PAML: a program package for phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Comput. Appl. Biosci. 13, 555â556 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4147">81</a></sup>) was used to determine the actual <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> value of each pair. To build the orchid paranome (all duplicated genes) <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> age distribution, a correction was performed as described in Maere <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Maere, S. et al. Modeling gene and genome duplications in eukaryotes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 102, 5454â5459 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4163">10</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Gene family evolution and phylogenomic dating.</h3><p>We used 72 single-copy families shared by all 11 species to construct a phylogenetic species tree. We applied the CafÃ© program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J.P. &amp; Hahn, M.W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269â1271 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4175">82</a></sup> to identify gene families that had undergone expansions or contractions.</p><p>To date the divergence times of orchid and other monocots, we used 342 single-copy gene families shared by orchid, the other 4 monocots (<i>O. sativa</i>, <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i> and <i>Z. mays</i>) and 3 eudicots (<i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>). The PAML MCMCTree program was used to estimate species divergence times, with the options 'correlated molecular clock' and 'JC69' model. The 'alpha' parameter was estimated by PhyML using the same set of sequence data. MCMC analysis was run for 20,000 generations, using a burn-in of 1,000 iterations. Other parameters were left at default settings. Phase 1 (non-degenerate) sequences for all single-copy gene families that were identified by the PLAZA pipeline were used as the input file for PhyML and MCMCTree. We used the <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>B. distachyon</i> divergence time (40â54 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763â768 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4210">83</a></sup>), the <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> divergence time (100â120 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4221">84</a></sup>) and the monocot and eudicot divergence time (130â240 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4225">11</a></sup>) as calibrators to predict the divergence time of other nodes and obtained a predicted divergence time of 135.1 million years ago (95% credibility interval of 118.1â152.7 million years ago) for <i>P. equestris</i> and cereals.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Evolutionary analysis of CAM genes.</h3><p>We identified putative CAM genes by searching the InterProScan result of all predicted <i>P. equestris</i> proteins. We identified orthologs of the <i>P. equestris</i> CAM genes in the genomes of other species, including <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>Z. mays</i>, <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>S. bicolor</i>, using a reciprocal best hit (RBH) strategy implemented with NCBI BLAST and custom scripts. We then aligned the coding sequences of each gene family using MUSCLE implemented in MEGA5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2731â2739 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4258">85</a>). Before constructing the phylogeny, we removed dubious short reading frames and obviously unrelated genes resulting from the relaxed annotation of InterProScan. A gene tree was then constructed with MEGA5 using maximum likelihood for each gene family.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">MADS-box gene analysis.</h3><p>MADS-box genes were identified by searching the InterProScan result of all predicted <i>P. equestris</i> proteins. MADS-box domains comprising 60 amino acids, identified by SMART<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Letunic, I. et al. Recent improvements to the SMART domain-based sequence annotation resource. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 242â244 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3149#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d46199e4274">86</a></sup> for all the MADS-box genes, were then aligned using ClustalW. An unrooted neighbor-joining phylogenetic tree was constructed in MEGA5 with default parameters. Bootstrap analysis was performed using 1,000 iterations.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">URLs.</h3><p>OrchidBase, <a href="http://orchidbase.itps.ncku.edu.tw/">http://orchidbase.itps.ncku.edu.tw/</a>; Angiosperm Phylogeny Website, <a href="http://www.mobot.org/MOBOT/research/APweb/">http://www.mobot.org/MOBOT/research/APweb/</a>; World Checklist of Orchidaceae, <a href="http://apps.kew.org/wcsp/">http://apps.kew.org/wcsp/</a>; RepeatProteinMask, <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/cgi-bin/RepeatProteinMaskRequest">http://www.repeatmasker.org/cgi-bin/RepeatProteinMaskRequest</a>; EMBOSS, <a href="http://emboss.sourceforge.net/">http://emboss.sourceforge.net/</a>; BESTORF, <a href="http://linux1.softberry.com/berry.phtml?topic=bestorf&amp;group=help&amp;subgroup=gfind">http://linux1.softberry.com/berry.phtml?topic=bestorf&amp;group=help&amp;subgroup=gfind</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Accession codes.</h3><p>Sequencing data, annotations and analyses results have all been uploaded to the FTP site <a href="ftp://ftp.genomics.org.cn/from_BGISZ/20130120/">ftp://ftp.genomics.org.cn/from_BGISZ/20130120/</a> for evaluation. The data have also been submitted to the NCBI database under BioProject <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA192198">PRJNA192198</a>.</p></div></div></section>

            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="change-history" data-title="Change history"><div class="c-article-section" id="change-history-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="change-history">Change history</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="change-history-content"><ul class="c-article-change-list"><li class="c-article-change-list__item" id="chg1"><ins datetime="2015-01-09"><h3 class="c-article-change-list__heading u-h3">09 January 2015</h3><div class="c-article-change-list__details"><p>In the version of this article initially published, the divergence time estimates in Figure 1b were incorrect, and the genome size estimation for <i>Phalaenopsis equestris</i> was incorrectly stated as 1.6 Ã 10<sup>6</sup> instead of the correct 1.6 Ã 10<sup>9</sup>. Finally, Figure 3 incorrectly showed maleic acid in the vacuole reaction, which should have been malic acid. The errors have been corrected in the HTML and PDF versions of the article.</p></div></ins></li><li class="c-article-change-list__item" id="chg2"><ins datetime="2015-02-06"><h3 class="c-article-change-list__heading u-h3">06 February 2015</h3><div class="c-article-change-list__details"><p>In the version of this article initially published, the legend for Figure 1b referred to red arrows indicating the inferred divergence dates. No arrows are depicted in the figure, so this sentence has been removed from the figure legend in the HTML and PDF versions of the article.</p></div></ins></li></ul></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Bib1" data-title="References"><div class="c-article-section" id="Bib1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Bib1">References</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Bib1-content"><div data-container-section="references"><ol class="c-article-references"><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="headline" content="Darwin, C. W.-C.T., Y.-Y.S., Z.-J.P., C.-C.H., Y.-P.Y., Y.-C.H., Y.-C.C., L.-J.C., X.-J.X., G.-Q.Z., M.W., G.-C.X., G.-H.L. and L.-Q.L. collected and grew the plant material. J.C., W.-C.T., K.-W.L., L.-J.C. and Q.X. prepared samples. X.L., C.B., Z.Z., W.L., F.S. and K.-W.L. sequenced and processed the raw data. X.L., C.B., Z.Z., X.X., J.W. and F.S. annotated the genome. C.B., X.L., J.C., Y.H. and S.P. analyzed the gene families. C.B., X.L., S.P., K.V., Y.H. and Z.Z. conducted genome evolution analysis. J.C. conducted CAM analysis. J.C., X.L. and F.-M.S. conducted TE insertion analysis. Z.-J.L. and J.-Y.W. conducted protein kinase analysis. W.-C.T., Y.-Y.H., Z.-J.P., C.-C.H., Y.-P.Y., Y.-C.H., Y.-C.C., A.D. and J.-F.D. conducted the MADS-box gene analysis. X.L., J.C. and K.-W.L. conducted transcriptome sequencing and analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.3149/email/correspondent/c1/new">Lai-Qiang Huang</a> or <a id="corresp-c2" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.3149/email/correspondent/c2/new">Yi-Bo Luo</a> or <a id="corresp-c3" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.3149/email/correspondent/c3/new">Hong-Hwa Chen</a> or <a id="corresp-c4" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.3149/email/correspondent/c4/new">Yves Van de Peer</a> or <a id="corresp-c5" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.3149/email/correspondent/c5/new">Zhong-Jian Liu</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Competing interests</h3>

                <i>Molecules</i>
                (2020)
              </p>
            </li>
          
        </ul>
      </div>
    </div>
    </section>
